# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Firefox SpiderMonkey JS Parsing to Bytecode Chain

## Your Task

Trace how JavaScript source text is parsed and compiled to bytecode in mozilla/gecko-dev SpiderMonkey. Find C++ source files implementing: the JS parser entry point, the AST node types, the bytecode emitter, and the JSScript bytecode output.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the crossrepo domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/firefox--871325b8.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/firefox--871325b8` (mozilla-firefox/firefox)

## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings in the following structure:

```json
{
  "files": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go"}
  ],
  "symbols": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "SymbolName"}
  ],
  "chain": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "FunctionName"}
  ],
  "text": "Narrative explanation of your findings, citing repos and file paths."
}
```

Include only the fields relevant to this task. Your answer is evaluated against a closed-world oracle — completeness matters.

- **File recall and precision**: Did you find all relevant files?

Perfect! I've created a comprehensive answer document that traces the entire JavaScript source parsing and compilation to bytecode chain in SpiderMonkey. Here's what I found:

## Summary of the Parsing and Compilation Chain

**1. Compilation Entry Points** (`js/src/frontend/BytecodeCompiler.h`):
- Functions like `CompileGlobalScript()`, `CompileModule()`, and `CompileEvalScript()` serve as entry points for the compilation pipeline

**2. Parser** (`js/src/frontend/Parser.h` and `Parser.cpp`):
- The `Parser` class (templated on handler type and character unit) tokenizes and parses JavaScript source
- Main entry methods: `globalBody()`, `evalBody()`, `moduleBody()` that produce an Abstract Syntax Tree (AST)

**3. AST Node Types** (`js/src/frontend/ParseNode.h`):
- `ParseNode` and its subclasses (UnaryNode, BinaryNode, ListNode, CallNode, FunctionNode, etc.) represent the parsed syntax tree
- Defined via the `FOR_EACH_PARSE_NODE_KIND` macro

**4. Bytecode Emitter** (`js/src/frontend/BytecodeEmitter.h` and `.cpp`):
- `BytecodeEmitter` traverses the AST via `emitScript()` method
- Generates bytecode instructions stored in `BytecodeSection`
- Creates intermediate Stencil representation

**5. Bytecode Definition** (`js/src/vm/Opcodes.h`):
- Defines all bytecode instructions via the `JSOp` enum
- Uses `FOR_EACH_OPCODE` macro to define instruction semantics (opcode length, stack effects, operand formats)

**6. Final Output** (`js/src/vm/JSScript.h`):
- `JSScript` class stores the compiled bytecode
- `code()` method returns pointer to immutable bytecode bytes
- `immutableScriptData()` provides full script metadata and bytecode

Certain limitations of\\n24:  * C++ (primarily the inability to partially specialize function templates),\\n25:  * plus the desire to minimize compiled code size in duplicate function\\n26:  * template instantiations wherever possible, mean that Parser exhibits much of\\n27:  * the same unholy template/inheritance complexity as token streams.\\n28:  *\\n29:  * == ParserSharedBase ==\\n30:  *\\n31:  * ParserSharedBase is the base class for both regular JS and BinAST parsing.\\n32:  * This class contains common fields and methods between both parsers. There is\\n33:  * currently no BinAST parser here so this can potentially be merged into the\\n34:  * ParserBase type below.\\n35:  *\\n36:  * == ParserBase \u2192 ParserSharedBase, ErrorReportMixin ==\\n37:  *\\n38:  * ParserBase is the base class for regular JS parser, shared by all regular JS\\n39:  * parsers of all character types and parse-handling behavior.  It stores\\n40:  * everything character- and handler-agnostic.\\n41:  *\\n42:  * ParserBase's most important field is the parser's token stream's\\n43:  * |TokenStreamAnyChars| component, for all tokenizing aspects that are\\n44:  * character-type-agnostic.  The character-type-sensitive components residing\\n45:  * in |TokenStreamSpecific| (see the comment near the top of TokenStream.h)\\n46:  * live elsewhere in this hierarchy.  These separate locations are the reason\\n47:  * for the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameter to |TokenStreamChars| and\\n48:  * |TokenStreamSpecific|.\\n49:  *\\n50:  * == PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> \u2192 ParserBase ==\\n51:  *\\n52:  * Certain parsing behavior varies between full parsing and syntax-only parsing\\n53:  * but does not vary across source-text character types.  For example, the work\\n54:  * to \\\"create an arguments object for a function\\\" obviously varies between\\n55:  * syntax and full parsing but (because no source characters are examined) does\\n56:  * not vary by source text character type.  Such functionality is implemented\\n57:  * through functions in PerHandlerParser.\\n58:  *\\n59:  * Functionality only used by syntax parsing or full parsing doesn't live here:\\n60:  * it should be implemented in the appropriate Parser<ParseHandler> (described\\n61:  * further below).\\n62:  *\\n63:  * == GeneralParser<ParseHandler, Unit> \u2192 PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> ==\\n64:  *\\n65:  * Most parsing behavior varies across the character-type axis (and possibly\\n66:  * along the full/syntax axis).  For example:\\n67:  *\\n68:  *   * Parsing ECMAScript's Expression production, implemented by\\n69:  *     GeneralParser::expr, varies in this manner: different types are used to\\n70:  *     represent nodes in full and syntax parsing (ParseNode* versus an enum),\\n71:  *     and reading the tokens comprising the expression requires inspecting\\n72:  *     individual characters (necessarily dependent upon character type).\\n73:  *   * Reporting an error or warning does not depend on the full/syntax parsing\\n74:  *     distinction.  But error reports and warnings include a line of context\\n75:  *     (or a slice of one), for pointing out where a mistake was made.\\n76:  *     Computing such line of context requires inspecting the source text to\\n77:  *     make that line/slice of context, which requires knowing the source text\\n78:  *     character type.\\n79:  *\\n80:  * Such functionality, implemented using identical function code across these\\n81:  * axes, should live in GeneralParser.\\n82:  *\\n83:  * GeneralParser's most important field is the parser's token stream's\\n84:  * |TokenStreamSpecific| component, for all aspects of tokenizing that (contra\\n85:  * |TokenStreamAnyChars| in ParserBase above) are character-type-sensitive.  As\\n86:  * noted above, this field's existence separate from that in ParserBase\\n87:  * motivates the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameters on various token stream\\n88:  * classes.\\n89:  *\\n90:  * Everything in PerHandlerParser *could* be folded into GeneralParser (below)\\n91:  * if desired.  We don't fold in this manner because all such functions would\\n92:  * be instantiated once per Unit -- but if exactly equivalent code would be\\n93:  * generated (because PerHandlerParser functions have no awareness of Unit),\\n94:  * it's risky to *depend* upon the compiler coalescing the instantiations into\\n95:  * one in the final binary.  PerHandlerParser guarantees no duplication.\\n96:  *\\n97:  * == Parser<ParseHandler, Unit> final \u2192 GeneralParser<ParseHandler, Unit> ==\\n98:  *\\n99:  * The final (pun intended) axis of complexity lies in Parser.\\n100:  *\\n101: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/firefox--871325b8@01dcdcebaca08ed5c32f8d50532752a063dc5ade/-/blob/js/src/frontend/Parser.h%3FL1-100\"}",

    "content": "1: /* -*- Mode: C++; tab-width: 8; indent-tabs-mode: nil; c-basic-offset: 2 -*-\n2:  * vim: set ts=8 sts=2 et sw=2 tw=80:\n3:  * This Source Code Form is subject to the terms of the Mozilla Public\n4:  * License, v. 2.0. Certain limitations of\n24:  * C++ (primarily the inability to partially specialize function templates),\n25:  * plus the desire to minimize compiled code size in duplicate function\n26:  * template instantiations wherever possible, mean that Parser exhibits much of\n27:  * the same unholy template/inheritance complexity as token streams.\n28:  *\n29:  * == ParserSharedBase ==\n30:  *\n31:  * ParserSharedBase is the base class for both regular JS and BinAST parsing.\n32:  * This class contains common fields and methods between both parsers. There is\n33:  * currently no BinAST parser here so this can potentially be merged into the\n34:  * ParserBase type below.\n35:  *\n36:  * == ParserBase \u2192 ParserSharedBase, ErrorReportMixin ==\n37:  *\n38:  * ParserBase is the base class for regular JS parser, shared by all regular JS\n39:  * parsers of all character types and parse-handling behavior.  It stores\n40:  * everything character- and handler-agnostic.\n41:  *\n42:  * ParserBase's most important field is the parser's token stream's\n43:  * |TokenStreamAnyChars| component, for all tokenizing aspects that are\n44:  * character-type-agnostic.  The character-type-sensitive components residing\n45:  * in |TokenStreamSpecific| (see the comment near the top of TokenStream.h)\n46:  * live elsewhere in this hierarchy.  These separate locations are the reason\n47:  * for the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameter to |TokenStreamChars| and\n48:  * |TokenStreamSpecific|.\n49:  *\n50:  * == PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> \u2192 ParserBase ==\n51:  *\n52:  * Certain parsing behavior varies between full parsing and syntax-only parsing\n53:  * but does not vary across source-text character types.  For example, the work\n54:  * to \"create an arguments object for a function\" obviously varies between\n55:  * syntax and full parsing but (because no source characters are examined) does\n56:  * not vary by source text character type. For example:\n67:  *\n68:  *   * Parsing ECMAScript's Expression production, implemented by\n69:  *     GeneralParser::expr, varies in this manner: different types are used to\n70:  *     represent nodes in full and syntax parsing (ParseNode* versus an enum),\n71:  *     and reading the tokens comprising the expression requires inspecting\n72:  *     individual characters (necessarily dependent upon character type).\n73:  *   * Reporting an error or warning does not depend on the full/syntax parsing\n74:  *     distinction. Operands named\\n59:  * `forwardOffset` or `defaultOffset` must be positive.\\n60:  *\\n61:  * Forward jumps must jump to a `JSOp::JumpTarget` instruction.  Backward jumps,\\n62:  * indicated by negative offsets, must jump to a `JSOp::LoopHead` instruction.\\n63:  * Jump offsets can't be zero.\\n64:  *\\n65:  * Needless to say, scripts must not contain overlapping instruction sequences\\n66:  * (in the sense of <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overlapping_gene>).\\n67:  *\\n68:  * A script's `trynotes` and `scopeNotes` impose further constraints. Each try\\n69:  * note and each scope note marks a region of the bytecode where some invariant\\n70:  * holds, or some cleanup behavior is needed--that there's a for-in iterator in\\n71:  * a particular stack slot, for instance, which must be closed on error. All\\n72:  * paths into the span must establish that invariant. In practice, this means\\n73:  * other code never jumps into the span: the only way in is to execute the\\n74:  * bytecode instruction that sets up the invariant (in our example,\\n75:  * `JSOp::Iter`).\\n76:  *\\n77:  * If a script's `trynotes` (see \\\"Try Notes\\\" in JSScript.h) contain a\\n78:  * `JSTRY_CATCH` or `JSTRY_FINALLY` span, there must be a `JSOp::Try`\\n79:  * instruction immediately before the span and a `JSOp::JumpTarget immediately\\n80:  * after it. Bytecode has many consumers, not just the interpreter: JITs,\n46:  * analyses, the debugger. That's why the rules below apply even to code that\n47:  * can't be reached in ordinary execution (such as code after an infinite loop\n48:  * or inside an `if (false)` block).\n49:  *\n50:  * The `code()` of a script must be a packed (not aligned) sequence of valid\n51:  * instructions from start to end. Each instruction has a single byte opcode\n52:  * followed by a number of operand bytes based on the opcode.\n53:  *\n54:  * ## Jump instructions\n55:  *\n56:  * Operands named `offset`, `forwardOffset`, or `defaultOffset` are jump\n57:  * offsets, the distance in bytes from the start of the current instruction to\n58:  * the start of another instruction in the same script. Operands named\n59:  * `forwardOffset` or `defaultOffset` must be positive.\n60:  *\n61:  * Forward jumps must jump to a `JSOp::JumpTarget` instruction.  Backward jumps,\n62:  * indicated by negative offsets, must jump to a `JSOp::LoopHead` instruction.\n63:  * Jump offsets can't be zero.\n64:  *\n65:  * Needless to say, scripts must not contain overlapping instruction sequences\n66:  * (in the sense of <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overlapping_gene>).\n67:  *\n68:  * A script's `trynotes` and `scopeNotes` impose further constraints. Each try\n69:  * note and each scope note marks a region of the bytecode where some invariant\n70:  * holds, or some cleanup behavior is needed--that there's a for-in iterator in\n71:  * a particular stack slot, for instance, which must be closed on error. All\n72:  * paths into the span must establish that invariant. In practice, this means\n73:  * other code never jumps into the span: the only way in is to execute the\n74:  * bytecode instruction that sets up the invariant (in our example,\n75:  * `JSOp::Iter`).\n76:  *\n77:  * If a script's `trynotes` (see \"Try Notes\" in JSScript.h) contain a\n78:  * `JSTRY_CATCH` or `JSTRY_FINALLY` span, there must be a `JSOp::Try`\n79:  * instruction immediately before the span and a `JSOp::JumpTarget immediately\n80:  * after it. Instructions must not jump to this `JSOp::JumpTarget`. (The VM puts\n81:  * us there on exception.) Furthermore, the instruction sequence immediately\n82:  * following a `JSTRY_CATCH` span must read `JumpTarget; Exception` or, in\n83:  * non-function scripts, `JumpTarget; Undefined; SetRval; Exception`. (These\n84:  * instructions run with an exception pending; other instructions aren't\n85:  * designed to handle that.)\n86:  *\n87:  * Unreachable instructions are allowed, but they have to follow all the rules.\n88:  *\n89:  * Control must not reach the end of a script. (Currently, the last instruction\n90:  * is always JSOp::RetRval.)\n91:  *\n92:  * ## Other operands\n93:  *\n94:  * Operands named `nameIndex` or `atomIndex` (which appear on instructions that\n95:  * have `JOF_ATOM` in the `format` field) must be valid indexes into\n96:  * `script->atoms()`.\n97:  *\n98:  * Operands named `argc` (`JOF_ARGC`) are argument counts for call\n99:  * instructions. `argc` must be small enough that the instruction's nuses is <=\n100:  * the current stack depth (see \"Stack depth\" below).\n101: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/firefox--871325b8@01dcdcebaca08ed5c32f8d50532752a063dc5ade/-/blob/js/src/vm/Opcodes.h%3FL1-100"
